reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, input_size, hidden_size, num_layers=3, bias=True, batch_first=False):
        """
        :param input_size: The number of expected features in the input x
        :param hidden_size: The number of features in the hidden state h
        :param num_layers: Number of recurrent layers (default: 1)
        :param bias: If False, then the layer does not use bias weights b_ih and b_hh (default: True)
        :param batch_first: If True, then the input and output tensors are provided as (batch, seq, feature) (default: False)
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        
        self.gru = nn.GRU(input_size, hidden_size, num_layers, bias, batch_first, dropout=0, bidirectional=True)
    
    def forward(self, x,h0):
        """
        :param x: The input tensor, shape (seq_len, batch_size, input_size) if batch_first=False, otherwise (batch_size, seq_len, input_size)
        :param h_0: The initial hidden state for the input sequence, shape (num_layers * num_directions, batch_size, hidden_size) (default: None)
        :return: output, h_n
            - output: The output features (h_t) from the last layer of the GRU, for each t, shape (seq_len, batch_size, num_directions * hidden_size) if batch_first=False, otherwise (batch_size, seq_len, num_directions * hidden_size)
            - h_n: The hidden state for t = seq_len, shape (num_layers * num_directions, batch_size, hidden_size)
        """
        output, h_n = self.gru(x, h0)
        return h_n

# Test code
batch_size = 10
seq_len = 512
input_size = 128
hidden_size = 256
num_layers = 6

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(seq_len, batch_size, input_size),torch.rand((num_layers*2, batch_size, hidden_size))]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [input_size, hidden_size, num_layers]

Actually, an easier approach for W_hh @ h: since hidden_size=256 and 3*hidden=768, we can just compute it using small tensor cores or direct multiply-add in registers. With 256 threads, each thread could compute 3 outputs (one per gate group) if we partition appropriately. Wait, let me count. If we have 256 threads and need to compute a matvec of size 768×256, each thread can compute 3 elements. Each element is a dot product of length 256. So each thread does 3 * 256 = 768 FMAs per timestep. For 512 timesteps, that's 393,216 FMAs per thread. For 10 batch elements, total FMAs = 3,932,160 per layer per direction. At 2 FLOPs per FMA... actually FMA is 2 FLOPs. So about 8M FLOPs, which is tiny. The issue is memory bandwidth. Each thread needs to read 3 * 256 = 768 weights per timestep. For 512 timesteps, that's 393,216 floats = 1.5 MB per batch element. For 10 batch elements, 15 MB. At 3.35 TB/s, that's about 4.5 microseconds just for weight reads, plus other memory. So theoretically quite fast. But the kernel needs to be well-structured to achieve this. If weights are read from global memory each time, coalescing matters. For W_hh stored in global memory as (768, 256), if thread i computes output j, it needs to read W_hh[j, :]. For coalesced access, threads in a warp should read consecutive elements. Let me try a different kernel design: ```cuda __global__ void gru_forward_kernel( const float* proj_ih, // (seq_len, batch, 3*hidden) const float* W_hh, // (3*hidden, hidden) const float* b_hh, // (3*hidden,) const float* h0, // (batch, hidden) float* h_n, // (batch, hidden) int seq_len, int batch, int hidden) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; // W_hh has size (3*hidden, hidden) // Each thread will compute a subset of the 3*hidden outputs // Load h for this batch element extern __shared__ float shared[]; // h[hidden] + W_hh_chunk[chunk_size][hidden] + gates[3*hidden] float* h = shared; float* w = shared + hidden; float* gates = w + chunk_size * hidden; // Initialize h from h0 for (int i = tid; i < hidden; i += num_threads) { h[i] = h0[b * hidden + i]; } __syncthreads(); for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; t++) { // Compute W_hh @ h in chunks for (int chunk = 0; chunk < 3*hidden; chunk += chunk_size) { int curr_chunk_size = min(chunk_size, 3*hidden - chunk); // Load W_hh[chunk:chunk+curr_chunk_size, :] into shared memory for (int idx = tid; idx < curr_chunk_size * hidden; idx += num_threads) { int row = idx / hidden; int col = idx % hidden; w[row * hidden + col] = W_hh[(chunk + row) * hidden + col]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute dot products for (int i = tid; i < curr_chunk_size; i += num_threads) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < hidden; j++) { sum += w[i * hidden + j] * h[j]; } gates[chunk + i] = sum; } __syncthreads(); } // Add proj_ih and b_hh const float* proj_t = proj_ih + t * batch * 3*hidden + b * 3*hidden; for (int i = tid; i < 3*hidden; i += num_threads) { gates[i] += proj_t[i] + b_hh[i]; } __syncthreads(); // Apply activations and compute new h for (int i = tid; i < hidden; i += num_threads) { float r = sigmoid(gates[i]); float z = sigmoid(gates[hidden + i]); float n = tanh(gates[2*hidden + i] + r * gates[2*hidden + i] - something); } } } ``` Wait, I need to recall the exact GRU formula. It depends on whether it's the PyTorch default (updated) or the older version. PyTorch GRU formula: ``` r_t = sigmoid(W_ir @ x_t + b_ir + W_hr @ h_{t-1} + b_hr) z_t = sigmoid(W_iz @ x_t + b_iz + W_hz @ h_{t-1} + b_hz) n_t = tanh(W_in @ x_t + b_in + r_t * (W_hn @ h_{t-1} + b_hn)) h_t = (1 - z_t) * n_t + z_t * h_{t-1} ``` So there are 6 weight matrices (W_ir, W_iz, W_in for input; W_hr, W_hz, W_hn for hidden) and 6 biases. In PyTorch's parameter layout, `weight_ih_l[k]` is a (3*hidden_size, input_size) tensor, and `weight_hh_l[k]` is a (3*hidden_size, hidden_size) tensor. The first hidden_size rows correspond to reset gate (r), the next to update gate (z), and the last to new gate (n). So: - `W_ir, W_iz, W_in` are in `weight_ih_l[k][0:hidden, :]`, `weight_ih_l[k][hidden:2*hidden, :]`, `weight_ih_l[k][2*hidden:3*hidden, :]` - Similarly for `W_hr, W_hz, W_hn` in `weight_hh_l[k]` And `b_ih` and `b_hh` are both (3*hidden,) tensors. So in our precomputed `proj_ih`, it should be: `proj_ih = x @ W_ih.T + b_ih` where W_ih is the concatenated weight. Actually, for better performance, we might want to do both `x @ W_ih.T` and `+ b_ih` in the same kernel or matmul. Now for the hidden projection: `proj_hh = h @ W_hh.T` which gives (batch, 3*hidden) if h is (batch, hidden). But wait, for the n gate, it's `r_t * (W_hn @ h_{t-1} + b_hn)`, not just add. So the precomputed hidden projection for n gets multiplied by r_t. In fact, the three hidden projections are: - `hr = W_hr @ h_{t-1}` - `hz = W_hz @ h_{t-1}` - `hn = W_hn @ h_{t-1}` Then: - r = sigmoid(ir + br + hr + bhr) - z = sigmoid(iz + bz + hz + bhz) - n = tanh(in + bn + r * (hn + bhn)) - h = (1-z) * n + z * h_{t-1} Where ir, iz, in are the input projections, and br, bz, bn are the input biases, and bhr, bhz, bhn are the hidden biases. Actually, PyTorch's GRUCell uses: `if bias_ih is not None: b_ih, b_hh = bias_ih, bias_hh else: b_ih, b_hh = 0, 0` `gi = F.linear(input, weight_ih, b_ih)` `gh = F.linear(hidden, weight_hh, b_hh)` `i_r, i_z, i_n = gi.chunk(3, 1)` `h_r, h_z, h_n = gh.chunk(3, 1)` `resetgate = sigmoid(i_r + h_r)` `inputgate = sigmoid(i_z + h_z)` `newgate = tanh(i_n + resetgate * h_n)` `hy = newgate + inputgate * (hidden - newgate)` So `gi` includes `b_ih`, and `gh` includes `b_hh`. At each timestep, each thread reads 256 floats from global memory. The reads for a given thread are perfectly coalesced within the warp (consecutive elements of the row). But are reads across threads coalesced? W_hh is stored as (768, 256) in row-major order. Thread j reads row j: W_hh[j, 0], W_hh[j, 1], ..., W_hh[j, 255]. Thread j+1 reads row j+1: W_hh[j+1, 0], etc. When thread j reads W_hh[j, k], thread j+1 reads W_hh[j+1, k]. These are separated by 256 floats (1024 bytes). In fact, it's a strided access with stride 256, which is terrible for memory bandwidth. So having each thread read a full row gives terrible memory access patterns. We need to fix this. Instead, we should have threads reading the matrix in a coalesced pattern. For example: - Thread i in a warp reads W_hh[:, i] elements. Same number! But with this design, warp 0 computes output 0 (sum of h[k] * W[0, k]), warp 1 computes output 1, etc. Within warp w, thread t computes `h[t] * W[w, t]`, `h[t+32] * W[w, t+32]`, etc. Actually, for coalesced access: in warp w, thread t reads W[w, t], W[w, t+32], W[w, t+64], ... For 768 outputs, we need 32 iterations. Per iteration: - Load one row of W into registers or shared memory? No, threads read elements as needed. - Each warp computes one output. Within the warp, thread t contributes partial products. At timestep, for output out_idx, warp w computes: `partial[t] = h[base + t] * W[out_idx, base + t]` for base in [0, 32, 64, ..., 224]. Each thread does 8 partial products. Then warp-level reduction (shfl) to sum. During this computation, for each base, thread t reads W[out_idx, base + t]. Threads 0..31 read W[out_idx, base] through W[out_idx, base+31], which are consecutive and perfectly coalesced! For 24 outputs and 8 bases, that's 24*8 = 192 coalesced reads per warp, but only 8 reads per thread. Actually, each thread reads 8 elements of W per output. For 24 outputs: 192 reads per thread per timestep. Each read is 4 bytes, so 768 bytes per thread per timestep. But coalesced across the warp. For 768 threads in 24 warps, total memory traffic per timestep: 768 outputs * 256 floats = 196,608 floats = 786,432 bytes. This is exactly the size of W_hh. With perfect coalescing, we get full memory bandwidth utilization. This is a great design! Let me formalize it: ```cuda __global__ void gru_recurrent_kernel( const float* proj_ih, // (seq_len, batch, 3*hidden) const float* W_hh, // (3*hidden, hidden) - row major const float* b_hh, // (3*hidden,) const float* h0, // (batch, hidden) float* h_out, // (batch, hidden) int seq_len, int batch, int hidden) { int b = blockIdx.x; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane_id = threadIdx.x % 32; int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; __shared__ float h_shared[256]; // hidden <= 256 __shared__ float gates[768]; // 3*hidden <= 768 __shared__ float h_out_shared[256]; // Load h0 for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < hidden; i += blockDim.x) { h_shared[i] = h0[b * hidden + i]; } __syncthreads(); for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; t++) { // Compute W_hh @ h_shared -> gates // Each warp handles one output for (int out_base = 0; out_base < 3*hidden; out_base += num_warps) { int out_idx = out_base + warp_id; if (out_idx < 3*hidden) { float sum = 0.0f; // Each lane handles multiple elements for (int k = lane_id; k < hidden; k += 32) { sum += W_hh[out_idx * hidden + k] * h_shared[k]; } // Warp reduce #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); } if (lane_id == 0) { gates[out_idx] = sum; } } } __syncthreads(); // Add proj_ih and b_hh const float* proj_t = proj_ih + t * batch * 3*hidden + b * 3*hidden; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < 3*hidden; i += blockDim.x) { gates[i] += proj_t[i] + b_hh[i]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute new h for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < hidden; i += blockDim.x) { float r = sigmoid(gates[i]); float z = sigmoid(gates[hidden + i]); float n = tanh(gates[2*hidden + i] + r * gates[2*hidden + i] - r * b_hh[2*hidden + i] + r * b_hh[2*hidden + i]); // Wait, let's be careful. gates[2*hidden + i] = W_hn @ h + b_hh_n + proj_in + b_ih_n // Actually, proj_ih already includes b_ih. And gates includes b_hh. // So gates[2*hidden + i] = proj_in + b_ih_n + W_hn @ h + b_hh_n // We need: n = tanh(proj_in + b_ih_n + r * (W_hn @ h + b_hh_n)) // = tanh(gates[2*hidden + i] - (W_hn @ h + b_hh_n) + r * (W_hn @ h + b_hh_n)) // = tanh(gates[2*hidden + i] + (r - 1) * (W_hn @ h + b_hh_n)) // Hmm, this is getting messy. Let me restructure. } } } ``` Actually, I realized the problem. In my gates computation, I computed `W_hh @ h + b_hh` for all 3*hidden features. But the new gate formula multiplies only the hidden part of the new gate (W_hn @ h + b_hh_n) by r_t. So if I precompute `gh = W_hh @ h + b_hh`, then: - r = sigmoid(ir + igr) where igr = gh[0:hidden] - z = sigmoid(iz + igz) where igz = gh[hidden:2*hidden] - But for n: n = tanh(in + r * (W_hn @ h + b_hh_n)) = tanh(in + r * (gh[2*hidden:3*hidden])) - Because gh[2*hidden:3*hidden] = W_hn @ h + b_hh_n. Let me write it cleaner: ```cuda __global__ void gru_recurrent_kernel( const float* proj_ih, const float* W_hh, const float* b_hh, const float* h0, float* h_out, int seq_len, int batch, int hidden) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane_id = tid % 32; const int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; // e.g., 24 for 768 threads const int three_hidden = hidden * 3; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* h = smem; // [hidden] float* gh = smem + hidden; // [three_hidden] // Load h0 for (int i = tid; i < hidden; i += blockDim.x) { h[i] = h0[b * hidden + i]; } __syncthreads(); for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; t++) { // Compute gh = W_hh @ h for (int out_base = 0; out_base < three_hidden; out_base += num_warps) { int out_idx = out_base + warp_id; if (out_idx < three_hidden) { float sum = 0.0f; // Strided access for coalescing: each lane reads W[out_idx, lane_id], W[out_idx, lane_id+32], ... for (int k = lane_id; k < hidden; k += 32) { sum += W_hh[out_idx * hidden + k] * h[k]; } // Warp reduce for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); } if (lane_id == 0) { gh[out_idx] = sum; } } } __syncthreads(); // Add b_hh and proj_ih, then compute activations and new h const float* proj_t = proj_ih + t * batch * three_hidden + b * three_hidden; for (int i = tid; i < hidden; i += blockDim.x) { float gi_r = proj_t[i]; float gi_z = proj_t[hidden + i]; float gi_n = proj_t[2 * hidden + i]; float gh_r = gh[i] + b_hh[i]; float gh_z = gh[hidden + i] + b_hh[hidden + i]; float gh_n = gh[2 * hidden + i] + b_hh[2 * hidden + i]; float r = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-(gi_r + gh_r))); float z = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-(gi_z + gh_z))); float n = tanhf(gi_n + r * gh_n); h[i] = (1.0f - z) * n + z * h[i]; } __syncthreads(); } // Write final h for (int i = tid; i < hidden; i += blockDim.x) { h_out[b * hidden + i] = h[i]; } } ``` Wait, for the num_warps to evenly divide or be used properly: if blockDim.x = 768, num_warps = 24. three_hidden = 768. During each iteration, threads in a warp read different elements of W and h. For consecutive out_idx values, the W rows are consecutive in memory (W[out_idx, :] and W[out_idx+1, :] are separated by hidden elements). For instance, each warp could compute multiple outputs using a different algorithm. For example, with 8 warps (256 threads), each warp computes 96 outputs (768/8). The warp computes output `warp_id + 8*j` for j=0..95. For a given k, the threads in the warp read W[warp_id + 8*j, k] for different j. These are strided by 8*256 floats = 8KB. That's terrible for memory coalescing. Alternatively, the threads in a warp could compute the same output but different k values. That's what my original design does. And with 8 warps, only 8 outputs are computed per iteration. So to reduce iterations, we need more warps. The minimum iterations is ceil(768 / num_warps) if each warp computes one output. To get 1 iteration, we'd need 768 warps = 24,576 threads, which is way too many. What if each thread computes multiple outputs? E.g., each thread in a warp computes 3 outputs (r, z, n for one hidden unit). Then 256 threads compute 256*3 = 768 outputs. But then W must be accessed differently. If thread j computes output j, j+256, j+512: - Thread j reads W[j, :], W[j+256, :], W[j+512, :] - Three separate dot products of length 256. - Total FMAs: 3 * 256 = 768 per thread per timestep. - For W access: thread j reads W[j, k], W[j+256, k], W[j+512, k] for each k. These are strided by 256 elements (1024 bytes). Consecutive threads read W[0, k], W[1, k], W[2, k] for their first output. That's perfectly coalesced! Wait, let me check: thread j reads for its first output: W[j, 0], W[j, 1], ..., W[j, 255] (sequentially in a loop). For a given k, threads 0..255 read W[0, k], W[1, k], ..., W[255, k]. These are consecutive in memory if W is row-major? No! W[row, col] for row-major: memory address = base + row * hidden + col. W[0, k], W[1, k], W[2, k], ... are at addresses base + 0*hidden + k, base + 1*hidden + k, base + 2*hidden + k, etc. These are strided by hidden floats (1024 bytes for hidden=256). NOT coalesced! For coalesced access, consecutive threads should read consecutive addresses. So thread j should read W[base_row, base_col + j] or similar. If W is stored as (hidden, 3*hidden) - i.e., transposed - then W[col, row] in the original corresponds to W_T[col*3*hidden + row]. Thread j reading W_T[k, j] gives address base + k*3*hidden + j, which IS consecutive for consecutive j. So maybe I should transpose W_hh for better memory access? If we store W_hh_T with shape (hidden, 3*hidden), then: gh[j] = sum_k h[k] * W_hh[j, k] = sum_k h[k] * W_hh_T[k, j] For coalesced access, for fixed k, threads j should read W_hh_T[k, j]. Address = base + k*3*hidden + j. Consecutive j gives consecutive addresses. Perfectly coalesced! Then each thread j computes gh[j] by looping over k and reading W_hh_T[k, j]. But each thread reads W_hh_T[0, j], W_hh_T[1, j], ..., W_hh_T[255, j]. For fixed j, these are strided by 3*hidden floats. That's 3KB stride. Again, not coalesced across threads for a given k, but consecutive threads DO read consecutive addresses for a given k! Wait, let's clarify: - Thread j, at iteration k, reads W_hh_T[k, j]. - All threads in a warp (j=0..31) at iteration k read W_hh_T[k, 0..31]. - Addresses: base + k*3*hidden + 0, base + k*3*hidden + 1, ..., base + k*3*hidden + 31. - These are perfectly consecutive. Perfectly coalesced! Great! So if we transpose W_hh, then each thread can compute one output feature with coalesced memory access. With this formulation: - gh[j] = sum_k h[k] * W_T[k*3*hidden + j] - Thread j computes this sum. - Number of threads needed = 768 for all outputs. - But we can use fewer threads! For example, 256 threads, where thread j computes outputs j, j+256, j+512. At iteration k: - Thread j reads W_T[k*3*hidden + j] for output j - Thread j reads W_T[k*3*hidden + j + 256] for output j+256 - Thread j reads W_T[k*3*hidden + j + 512] for output j+512 For a warp, at a given k and for a given output set (say output j): - Threads 0..31 read W_T[k*3*hidden + 0..31]. Coalesced. - For output j+256: threads read W_T[k*3*hidden + 256..287]. Coalesced. - For output j+512: threads read W_T[k*3*hidden + 512..543]. Coalesced. Wonderful! This means 256 threads can compute all 768 outputs. Each thread does 3 dot products of length 256, total 768 FMAs per thread per timestep. Same total FMAs as before, but now with 256 threads instead of 768, and perfectly coalesced memory access! So the optimal block design is: - 256 threads per block - 10 blocks (one per batch element) - Each block processes all timesteps - W_hh stored as (hidden, 3*hidden) = transposed Let me write this kernel: ```cuda __global__ void gru_recurrent_kernel( const float* proj_ih, // (seq_len, batch, 3*hidden) const float* W_hh_T, // (hidden, 3*hidden) - transposed! const float* b_hh, // (3*hidden,) const float* h0, // (batch, hidden) float* h_out, // (batch, hidden) int seq_len, int batch, int hidden) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int three_hidden = hidden * 3; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* h = smem; // [hidden] float* gh = smem + hidden; // [three_hidden] // Load h0 for (int i = tid; i < hidden; i += blockDim.x) { h[i] = h0[b * hidden + i]; } __syncthreads(); for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; t++) { // Compute gh = W_hh @ h // W_hh_T is (hidden, 3*hidden) // gh[j] = sum_k h[k] * W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + j] // Each thread computes 3 outputs: j, j+256, j+512 float sum_r = 0.0f, sum_z = 0.0f, sum_n = 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < hidden; k++) { float h_k = h[k]; float w_r = W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + tid]; float w_z = W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + tid + hidden]; float w_n = W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + tid + 2 * hidden]; sum_r += h_k * w_r; sum_z += h_k * w_z; sum_n += h_k * w_n; } gh[tid] = sum_r; gh[tid + hidden] = sum_z; gh[tid + 2 * hidden] = sum_n; __syncthreads(); const float* proj_t = proj_ih + t * batch * three_hidden + b * three_hidden; for (int i = tid; i < hidden; i += blockDim.x) { float gi_r = proj_t[i]; float gi_z = proj_t[i + hidden]; float gi_n = proj_t[i + 2 * hidden]; float gh_r = gh[i] + b_hh[i]; float gh_z = gh[i + hidden] + b_hh[i + hidden]; float gh_n = gh[i + 2 * hidden] + b_hh[i + 2 * hidden]; float r = sigmoid(gi_r + gh_r); float z = sigmoid(gi_z + gh_z); float n = tanhf(gi_n + r * gh_n); h[i] = (1.0f - z) * n + z * h[i]; } __syncthreads(); } for (int i = tid; i < hidden; i += blockDim.x) { h_out[b * hidden + i] = h[i]; } } ``` Wait, there's a subtlety. Thread tid computes gh[tid], gh[tid+hidden], gh[tid+2*hidden]. But then in the second phase, threads read gh[i], gh[i+hidden], gh[i+2*hidden] for i from tid to hidden-1 stepping by blockDim.x. If blockDim.x = 256 and hidden = 256, then tid iterates once with i=tid. So thread tid reads gh[tid], gh[tid+256], gh[tid+512]. Perfect, this matches what it wrote! But we need __syncthreads() between the write and read. Actually, since every thread writes to gh and then every thread reads from gh, we need __syncthreads(). We have one at the end of the gh computation. Good. Wait, but some threads might write to gh while others haven't finished the write. Since all threads in the block participate in the write loop and the loop bounds don't depend on thread ID in a divergent way (tid goes from 0 to 255, each does 3 writes), all threads reach __syncthreads() after writing. Good. Now, let's verify coalescing for W_hh_T: At iteration k, thread tid reads W_hh_T[k*three_hidden + tid], W_hh_T[k*three_hidden + tid + hidden], W_hh_T[k*three_hidden + tid + 2*hidden]. For a warp of 32 threads (tid=0..31), the first reads are: W_hh_T[k*three_hidden + 0], W_hh_T[k*three_hidden + 1], ..., W_hh_T[k*three_hidden + 31]. These are 32 consecutive floats = 128 bytes. Perfectly coalesced into 2 64-byte cache lines. Similarly for the second and third reads (offset by 256 and 512). Also consecutive within each group, so coalesced. Great! And each thread reads 3 floats per iteration, 256 iterations = 768 reads = 3KB per thread. Total reads per block: 256 threads * 768 reads * 4 bytes = 786 KB. For 10 blocks = 7.86 MB per timestep. Over 512 timesteps... wait no, W_hh_T is read 512 times per block, but it's the same data each time. So total W reads = 786 KB * 512 = 402 MB per block * 10 = 4 GB per layer per direction. At 3.35 TB/s, that's about 1.2 ms. Plus proj_ih reads and h writes. This is still memory bound but at least we're saturating bandwidth and not wasting work. Now, one optimization: if W_hh_T is small enough to stay in L2 cache across timesteps, subsequent reads might be much faster than going to HBM. 786 KB per block, and with 10 blocks, total working set is ~8 MB. The H100 L2 cache is probably 50+ MB. So W_hh_T might stay in L2 cache! This would significantly speed up the reads after the first few timesteps. Actually, the L2 cache is shared across SMs. 8 MB is quite small compared to L2 cache, so it should fit. The first timestep loads W_hh_T into L2, and subsequent timesteps hit L2. This would make the kernel much faster than the naive bandwidth calculation. Similarly, the kernel code is small and each block processes all timesteps locally, so there's good data reuse. Another optimization: loop unrolling. We can unroll the k loop by some factor to reduce loop overhead. But 256 iterations might not need unrolling, and unrolling by too much increases code size. I'll let the compiler handle it or add #pragma unroll with a small factor. Actually, with the `-O3 --use_fast_math` flags, the compiler should optimize well. Now let me think about the overall architecture again. For each layer/direction: 1. proj_ih = F.linear(x, W_ih, b_ih) [one matmul] 2. h_n = gru_recurrent_forward(proj_ih, W_hh_T, b_hh, h0) [custom kernel] For bidirectional layer 1: - Input x has shape (512, 10, 128) - Forward direction uses x - Backward direction uses x reversed For the backward direction: we can reverse x with `x.flip(0)` and then reverse the output back. Actually, reversing in PyTorch: `x_rev = x.flip(0)` creates a strided tensor. `F.linear(x_rev, W_ih, b_ih)` might work with the strided tensor, or we might need to make it contiguous. `flip(0)` is not a view; it creates a copy or uses negative strides. In newer PyTorch, `flip` returns a view with negative strides, but some operations require contiguous tensors. To avoid issues, we can use `torch.flip(x, [0]).contiguous()` for the backward direction. But this requires a memory copy of x (512*10*128*4 = 2.5 MB), which is small. For a standard RNN/GRU, the recurrence is not reversible in general because of the nonlinearities. Actually, another approach: compute proj_ih for the forward direction normally, and for the backward direction, compute on the original x but the kernel reads in reverse. Either works. Reading in reverse might have slightly worse memory coalescing for proj_ih. For proj_ih, reading `proj_ih + t * batch * three_hidden + b * three_hidden` where t decreases: the memory access is sequential across timesteps but in reverse. This is still coalesced within a warp (since all threads in a block access the same t but different b or different offsets). Actually, for a given block (fixed b), all threads read from the same t but different offsets within the three_hidden dimension. The offsets are `i`, `i+hidden`, `i+2*hidden` where i = tid to hidden-1 stepping by 256. For hidden=256 and blockDim=256, each thread reads 3 elements that are 256 apart. So thread 0 reads offsets 0, 256, 512. Thread 1 reads 1, 257, 513. These are consecutive within each group. So coalesced. As t decreases, we're just reading different 768-element chunks. The chunks are contiguous in memory (each chunk is `batch * three_hidden` floats = 10 * 768 * 4 = 30,720 bytes apart). So reverse iteration still accesses memory in large aligned chunks. Cache prefetchers might not like reverse order as much, but it should still be fine. To avoid any issues, we can reverse proj_ih with `torch.flip(proj_ih, [0])` outside the kernel, so the kernel always iterates forward. For the backward direction, we'd do: ```python proj_ih_back = torch.flip(F.linear(x, W_ih_back, b_ih_back), [0]) ``` Wait, `F.linear(x, W_ih, b_ih)` gives (seq_len, batch, 3*hidden). To reverse along seq_len: `torch.flip(proj_ih, dims=[0])`. But `torch.flip` creates a view with negative stride. The kernel reading this might still work fine since our access is regular. However, `torch.flip` doesn't make the tensor non-contiguous in the flipped dimension. The memory layout is still the same, just the stride changes. Our kernel accesses memory based on pointer arithmetic (`t * batch * three_hidden`), which assumes positive stride of `batch * three_hidden`. If the stride is negative, this assumption breaks! With `torch.flip(proj_ih, [0])`, the stride in dimension 0 becomes negative. So `proj_ih[0]` in the flipped tensor is actually `proj_ih[-1]` in memory. Our kernel reading `proj_ih + 0` would read the first element in memory, which would be the first timestep, not the last. To fix this, we'd need to either: 1. Make it contiguous: `torch.flip(proj_ih, [0]).contiguous()` - this is a full memory copy 2. Pass the stride to the kernel and compute the correct offset 3. Read in reverse in the kernel Option 3 is simplest. Let's just read in reverse for the backward direction within the kernel. Actually, we could just pass a boolean flag: ```cuda __global__ void gru_recurrent_kernel( const float* proj_ih, const float* W_hh_T, const float* b_hh, const float* h0, float* h_out, int seq_len, int batch, int hidden, bool reverse) { ... for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; t++) { int t_idx = reverse ? (seq_len - 1 - t) : t; const float* proj_t = proj_ih + t_idx * batch * three_hidden + b * three_hidden; ... } } ``` This is simple and correct. The compiler can handle the conditional easily since it's the same for all threads in the block. Wait, but if `reverse` is a runtime boolean, the compiler might not optimize it away. The host wrapper would need to call the correct instantiation. Actually, it can work if we have two wrapper functions or one wrapper that checks a boolean and dispatches. Now, let me think about another optimization: persistent kernel across multiple batches. Currently each block handles one batch element. But the batch dimension is independent. Can we have one block handle multiple batch elements to better utilize compute? For example, with blockDim=256, can we process 4 batch elements? We'd divide the 256 threads: 64 threads per batch element. Each sub-group of 64 threads computes 64 outputs at a time... 768 outputs / 64 = 12 iterations. That might be okay. With 10 blocks total, we'd occupy about 2 SMs fully, and the rest idle. That's terrible. To improve occupancy, we need to process multiple layer/directions in the same kernel launch or use multiple streams. Still not enough to fill the GPU. For 20 blocks with 256 threads each = 5120 threads. H100 might support 2048 threads per SM, so 3 SMs fully occupied. Still many SMs idle. But the kernel is memory-bound, not compute-bound. And memory bandwidth is shared across the entire GPU. So as long as we generate enough memory transactions to saturate the HBM bandwidth, we don't need to fill all SMs. The question is: can 20 blocks saturate 3.35 TB/s? Each block reads W_hh_T: 256 * 768 * 4 = 786 KB per timestep. For 512 timesteps: 402 MB per block. At 3.35 TB/s, one block takes ~120 microseconds for W reads if reading from HBM. But with L2 cache, it's much less. Actually, for the first few timesteps, W is read from HBM into L2. For subsequent timesteps, it's read from L2. So the effective bandwidth is much higher. 20 blocks would collectively read 20 * 786 KB = 15.4 MB per timestep. Over 512 timesteps, but with L2 cache, the total HBM traffic is just loading the 8 MB of weights a few times. So total HBM traffic is small. The main HBM traffic is reading proj_ih and writing h_out. For one layer/direction: - proj_ih: 512 * 10 * 768 * 4 = 15.7 MB - h_out: 10 * 256 * 4 = 10 KB For 2 directions: 31.4 MB + 20 KB. At 3.35 TB/s, that's ~9 microseconds. Plus the actual compute. So the overall time might be dominated by compute rather than HBM bandwidth, once L2 caches the weights. This means we should focus on compute efficiency. The kernel as written does 768 FMAs per thread per timestep, in a loop of 256 iterations. That's 196,608 FMAs per thread. For 256 threads per block, that's ~50M FMAs per block. For 20 blocks, ~1B FMAs. At 67 TFLOPS, that's ~15 microseconds. Plus instruction overhead, memory latency, etc. Wait, but can the kernel achieve full throughput? The FMAs are in a reduction loop. Each iteration does: 1. Load h[k] (shared memory, broadcast to all threads) 2. Load 3 weights (coalesced global/L2) 3. 3 FMAs With 256 threads doing this in parallel, we have 768 FMAs per cycle ideally. But `h[k]` is loaded from shared memory. Since all threads read the same `h[k]`, it's a broadcast, which is fast on shared memory. The weights are read from global/L2 with coalesced access. So the arithmetic intensity is: 768 FMAs / (3 loads + 1 broadcast load) ≈ 192 FLOPs per load. With L2 bandwidth much higher than HBM, this is compute-bound. Actually, with tensor cores, the throughput would be even higher. But we're not using tensor cores here. Given these back-of-the-envelope calculations, the kernel might be quite fast, perhaps comparable to or faster than cuDNN for this specific case. But I need to be careful about the exact PyTorch formula. Let me verify. PyTorch GRU formula (from docs): ``` r_t = sigmoid(W_ir @ x_t + b_ir + W_hr @ h_{t-1} + b_hr) z_t = sigmoid(W_iz @ x_t + b_iz + W_hz @ h_{t-1} + b_hz) n_t = tanh(W_in @ x_t + b_in + r_t * (W_hn @ h_{t-1} + b_hn)) h_t = (1 - z_t) * n_t + z_t * h_{t-1} ``` Where `W_ir, W_iz, W_in` are slices of `weight_ih_l0`, and `W_hr, W_hz, W_hn` are slices of `weight_hh_l0`. In my kernel, I have: - `proj_ih` = x @ W_ih.T + b_ih. This gives three components: gi_r, gi_z, gi_n. - `gh_r = W_hr @ h + b_hr`, etc. - `r = sigmoid(gi_r + gh_r)` ✓ - `z = sigmoid(gi_z + gh_z)` ✓ - `n = tanh(gi_n + r * gh_n)` ✓ - `h = (1-z)*n + z*h` ✓ Great, it matches. Now for h_n in the PyTorch reference. The model returns `h_n`, which has shape `(num_layers * num_directions, batch, hidden_size)`. For bidirectional, the first dimension is ordered as: `layer0_forward, layer0_backward, layer1_forward, layer1_backward, ...`. Or more precisely, for `nn.GRU` with num_layers layers: - h_n shape: (num_layers * 2, batch, hidden) - h_n[0] = final hidden of layer 0 forward - h_n[1] = final hidden of layer 0 backward - h_n[2] = final hidden of layer 1 forward - etc. In my custom implementation, I need to collect all these h_n values and stack them in the same order. Now for the backward direction, does `h_n` correspond to h after processing the first element? Yes, in PyTorch's bidirectional GRU, for the backward direction, the initial hidden state is h0[1], and the final hidden state is after processing x[0] (the first element of the original sequence). So in our kernel, we process from t=seq_len-1 down to 0, starting with h0. The final h after t=0 is the correct h_n for the backward direction. Alright, let me now draft the full implementation. Actually, I want to think about one more thing: can we process both directions in a single kernel launch per layer? For a single layer, we have 2 directions, each with its own W_ih, W_hh, b_ih, b_hh, h0. The inputs are the same but one direction reverses it. We could launch 2×batch blocks, where block (d, b) handles direction d and batch element b. But the directions have different weights and possibly different proj_ih. If we pass all these to a single kernel, it becomes more complex but saves launch overhead. With 12 kernel launches total (6 layers * 2 directions, if sequential), or 6 kernel launches (if both directions in one), the overhead might matter. For 6 layers with 2 directions per layer, and using streams for parallelism: - Layer 1: launch 2 kernels (forward, backward) on 2 streams, sync - Concatenate outputs -> input for layer 2 - Layer 2: launch 2 kernels, sync - ... Wait, we don't need to sync streams between layers. We just need to ensure layer 2 starts after layer 1 finishes. We can use `torch.cuda.synchronize()` or event-based synchronization. But in practice, if we use the default stream and sequential Python execution, it automatically serializes. Python-level: ```python def forward(self, x, h0): current = x h_n_list = [] for layer in range(self.num_layers): h0_f = h0[layer * 2] h0_b = h0[layer * 2 + 1] W_ih_f = ... W_hh_f = ... b_ih_f = ... b_hh_f = ... W_ih_b = ... ... proj_ih_f = F.linear(current, W_ih_f, b_ih_f) proj_ih_b = F.linear(current, W_ih_b, b_ih_b) h_n_f = gru_cuda.gru_recurrent_forward(proj_ih_f, W_hh_f, b_hh_f, h0_f, False) h_n_b = gru_cuda.gru_recurrent_forward(proj_ih_b, W_hh_b, b_hh_b, h0_b, True) h_n_list.append(h_n_f) h_n_list.append(h_n_b) # Compute output sequence for next layer # But wait, we only need h_n, not the full output sequence. # The next layer needs the full concatenated sequence as input. # So we need to compute the full output for timesteps. # Our kernel only returns h_n, not the output sequence! ``` Oh no! I made a critical mistake. The model returns only `h_n`, but the next layer needs the full output sequence from the current layer. We can't just compute h_n; we need the hidden states at all timesteps to feed into the next layer. But wait, the reference forward returns `h_n` only. But internally, `self.gru(x, h0)` computes both `output` and `h_n`. The `output` is needed between layers. Since we're replacing the forward computation, we DO need to compute the full output sequence layer by layer. However, the Python test code only calls `get_inputs()` and `forward()` and checks the returned `h_n`. It doesn't check the `output`. But to compute `h_n` correctly for layers 2 and above, we still need to compute the full sequence for layers below. Wait, actually no. For a multi-layer GRU, `h_n` for layer k depends on processing the full input sequence through layer k, which requires the output sequence from layer k-1. So we must compute the full output sequence for each layer (or at least enough to get the inputs for the next layer). This means our custom kernel needs to return either: 1. The full output sequence (seq_len, batch, hidden) for each direction, or 2. We need to write the output sequence to an intermediate buffer. But the model's forward only returns `h_n`. So while we need to compute the intermediate output sequences, we don't need to return them. This means I need to modify my kernel to store all hidden states, or I need a separate kernel that returns the full output. Actually, storing all hidden states for a layer and direction: (512, 10, 256) = 5,242,880 floats ≈ 20 MB. For 2 directions per layer and 6 layers, that's 240 MB total if all stored simultaneously. But we only need to store 2 directions at a time (one layer's outputs), so 40 MB. This is fine. And then for the next layer, the input is the concatenation: (512, 10, 512). So my kernel should be modified to store all h_t values: ```cuda __global__ void gru_forward_kernel( const float* proj_ih, const float* W_hh_T, const float* b_hh, const float* h0, float* output, // (seq_len, batch, hidden) float* h_out, // (batch, hidden) int seq_len, int batch, int hidden, bool reverse) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int three_hidden = hidden * 3; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* h = smem; for (int i = tid; i < hidden; i += blockDim.x) { h[i] = h0[b * hidden + i]; } __syncthreads(); for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; t++) { int t_idx = reverse ? (seq_len - 1 - t) : t; // Compute gh // ... (same as before) // Apply GRU and update h // ... // Store h to output at t_idx for (int i = tid; i < hidden; i += blockDim.x) { output[t_idx * batch * hidden + b * hidden + i] = h[i]; } __syncthreads(); } for (int i = tid; i < hidden; i += blockDim.x) { h_out[b * hidden + i] = h[i]; } } ``` Now the output buffer stores h_t for each timestep. For the backward direction, we store at `t_idx` (the original timestep index), so when the next layer reads it, it gets the correct concatenated input. Wait, for the next layer, the input should be the concatenation of forward output and backward output at each timestep. That is: `next_input[t] = cat([output_f[t], output_b[t]], dim=-1)` In PyTorch: `torch.cat([output_f, output_b], dim=-1)` where output_f and output_b are both (seq_len, batch, hidden). The result is (seq_len, batch, 2*hidden). We can do this concatenation on the GPU efficiently. So the overall forward pass becomes: ```python def forward(self, x, h0): current = x h_n_list = [] for layer in range(self.num_layers): h0_f = h0[layer * 2] h0_b = h0[layer * 2 + 1] W_ih_f = self.weight_ih_f[layer] W_hh_f = self.weight_hh_f[layer] b_ih_f = self.bias_ih_f[layer] if self.bias else None b_hh_f = self.bias_hh_f[layer] if self.bias else None W_ih_b = self.weight_ih_b[layer] W_hh_b = self.weight_hh_b[layer] b_ih_b = self.bias_ih_b[layer] if self.bias else None b_hh_b = self.bias_hh_b[layer] if self.bias else None proj_ih_f = F.linear(current, W_ih_f, b_ih_f) proj_ih_b = F.linear(current, W_ih_b, b_ih_b) output_f = torch.empty(seq_len, batch, hidden, device=x.device) output_b = torch.empty(seq_len, batch, hidden, device=x.device) h_n_f = torch.empty(batch, hidden, device=x.device) h_n_b = torch.empty(batch, hidden, device=x.device) gru_cuda.gru_forward(proj_ih_f, W_hh_f_T, b_hh_f, h0_f, output_f, h_n_f, False) gru_cuda.gru_forward(proj_ih_b, W_hh_b_T, b_hh_b, h0_b, output_b, h_n_b, True) h_n_list.append(h_n_f) h_n_list.append(h_n_b) current = torch.cat([output_f, output_b], dim=-1) h_n = torch.stack(h_n_list, dim=0) return h_n ``` This should be correct. But there's one more detail: for the backward direction, the output at timestep t should be the hidden state after processing x_t in the backward pass. For layer 0, h0[0] is for forward, h0[1] is for backward. For layer 1, h0[2] is for forward, h0[3] is for backward, etc. Yes. Now let me think about whether there's any way to optimize the concat between layers. Concatenating `output_f` and `output_b` on the last dimension can be done with `torch.cat`, but we could also write a custom kernel for it. However, `torch.cat` is well-optimized, so it's probably fine. Alternatively, we could fuse the concat into the kernel. For each timestep, after computing forward and backward h, we concatenate them. That would save a separate kernel launch and memory bandwidth. But it requires launching both directions and having them write to adjacent memory, which is hard without synchronization. Actually, for the next layer's `current` tensor, if we allocate it as (seq_len, batch, 2*hidden), then direction forward writes to `current[:, :, 0:hidden]` and backward writes to `current[:, :, hidden:2*hidden]`. These are independent writes, so we can launch both kernels pointing to different regions of the same buffer. ```python current = torch.empty(seq_len, batch, 2 * hidden, device=x.device) gru_cuda.gru_forward(proj_ih_f, ..., current[:, :, 0:hidden], h_n_f, False) gru_cuda.gru_forward(proj_ih_b, ..., current[:, :, hidden:2*hidden], h_n_b, True) ``` Wait, but `current[:, :, 0:hidden]` creates a view. Passing it to CUDA should work if the view is contiguous in the last dimension. For a tensor of shape (seq_len, batch, 2*hidden), a slice `[:, :, 0:hidden]` is contiguous if the last dimension is contiguous. The stride would be (batch*2*hidden, 2*hidden, 1). This slice has stride (batch*2*hidden, 2*hidden, 1), which is NOT contiguous as a 3D tensor because the stride of the last dimension is 1 but the second-to-last is 2*hidden while the size is batch and the inner dimension is hidden. Actually, for a tensor of shape (512, 10, 512) and stride (5120, 512, 1): - Slice [:, :, 0:256] has shape (512, 10, 256) and stride (5120, 512, 1). - The total size is 512 * 10 * 256 = 1,310,720 elements. - The memory layout: element [i, j, k] is at offset i*5120 + j*512 + k. - This means within a batch, consecutive k values are contiguous. Good. - But to write to this from CUDA, if we assume standard row-major layout, we need the pitch or stride of dimensions. Our kernel currently computes offsets as `t_idx * batch * hidden + b * hidden + i`. This assumes stride[0] = batch * hidden and stride[1] = hidden. For the slice with stride (5120, 512, 1), if we treat it as a 3D tensor with the kernel's flat indexing, we'd compute: - `t_idx * batch * hidden + b * hidden + i = t_idx * 10 * 256 + b * 256 + i` - But the actual offset should be `t_idx * 5120 + b * 512 + i` - These don't match! So we can't use the simple flat indexing unless we pass strides. We'd need to modify the kernel to accept strides for the output. Actually, that's not too bad. We can pass `seq_len, batch, hidden, out_stride0, out_stride1` and compute `output[t_idx * out_stride0 + b * out_stride1 + i]`. For the forward direction in the concatenated buffer: - out_stride0 = batch * 2 * hidden - out_stride1 = 2 * hidden - We write to indices `t_idx * batch * 2 * hidden + b * 2 * hidden + i`, which equals `t_idx * out_stride0 + b * out_stride1 + i`. For the backward direction: - Same strides, but writes to `t_idx * out_stride0 + b * out_stride1 + hidden + i`. This requires adding an offset parameter to the kernel! That is, a base pointer offset for where to write in the output. Let's modify the kernel: ```cuda __global__ void gru_forward_kernel( const float* proj_ih, const float* W_hh_T, const float* b_hh, const float* h0, float* output, int out_stride0, int out_stride1, int out_offset, float* h_out, int seq_len, int batch, int hidden, bool reverse) { ... for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; t++) { int t_idx = reverse ? (seq_len - 1 - t) : t; ... for (int i = tid; i < hidden; i += blockDim.x) { output[t_idx * out_stride0 + b * out_stride1 + out_offset + i] = h[i]; } __syncthreads(); } ... } ``` Then in Python: ```python current = torch.empty(seq_len, batch, 2 * hidden, device=x.device) gru_cuda.gru_forward(proj_ih_f, W_hh_f_T, b_hh_f, h0_f, current, seq_len*batch*2*hidden, batch*2*hidden, 0, h_n_f, seq_len, batch, hidden, False) gru_cuda.gru_forward(proj_ih_b, W_hh_b_T, b_hh_b, h0_b, current, seq_len*batch*2*hidden, batch*2*hidden, hidden, h_n_b, seq_len, batch, hidden, True) ``` Wait, the out_stride0 should be the actual stride in elements, not the total size. The stride of dimension 0 in elements is `current.stride(0)` = batch * 2 * hidden. Yes. Actually, `current.stride(0)` gives the number of elements to skip when moving by 1 in dimension 0. So `t_idx * current.stride(0)` is the correct offset. Similarly, `current.stride(1)` is `2 * hidden` for batch dimension. And `out_offset` is the offset within the last dimension, which is 0 for forward and hidden for backward. This is a good optimization because it avoids the separate `cat` operation and allows both kernels to write to the same tensor without any extra memory movement. But there's a catch: the two kernel launches run sequentially in the default stream. One writes to `current[:, :, 0:hidden]` and the other to `current[:, :, hidden:]`. They don't overlap, but they also don't need to synchronize. Actually, we could launch them on different streams to potentially overlap. But for now, sequential launching is simpler. For `F.linear(current, W_ih, b_ih)`, the result is (seq_len, batch, 3*hidden). Then the kernel reads `proj_ih + t_idx * batch * 3*hidden + b * 3*hidden + i`. We can pass `proj_ih.stride(0)` if we want, but for standard contiguous tensors, `stride(0) = batch * 3*hidden`. To be safe, we should compute the actual strides. But if `F.linear` returns a contiguous tensor, we can use the simple formula. Actually, `F.linear` with 3D input and bias returns a contiguous tensor if the input is contiguous. For layer 0, x is contiguous. For subsequent layers, `current` is contiguous by construction (we allocate it contiguous and write to it). But wait, when we do `gru_forward` writing to `current`, we need `current` to be contiguous so that `F.linear(current, ...)` is efficient. We just need to extract them. Actually, let me look at PyTorch GRU parameter names for bidirectional: - `weight_ih_l0` - input-to-hidden for layer 0 forward - `weight_hh_l0` - hidden-to-hidden for layer 0 forward - `bias_ih_l0`, `bias_hh_l0` - biases for layer 0 forward - `weight_ih_l0_reverse` - input-to-hidden for layer 0 backward - `weight_hh_l0_reverse` - hidden-to-hidden for layer 0 backward - `bias_ih_l0_reverse`, `bias_hh_l0_reverse` - biases for layer 0 backward - `weight_ih_l1` for layer 1 forward, etc. So in code: ```python for layer in range(self.num_layers): suffixes = ['', '_reverse'] for d, suffix in enumerate(suffixes): W_ih = getattr(self.gru, f'weight_ih_l{layer}{suffix}') W_hh = getattr(self.gru, f'weight_hh_l{layer}{suffix}') b_ih = getattr(self.gru, f'bias_ih_l{layer}{suffix}', None) b_hh = getattr(self.gru, f'bias_hh_l{layer}{suffix}', None) # Store W_ih, W_hh_T, b_ih, b_hh ``` Wait, when bias=False, these bias attributes don't exist. Using `getattr(..., None)` handles this. Now for the CUDA signature, I'll have the host wrapper take torch tensors directly. One more detail: should I use `float` or `half`? Now, there's another consideration: `load_inline` with `cuda_sources`. The C++ wrapper should use `at::Tensor` from `torch/extension.h`. Let me write the host wrapper: ```cuda torch::Tensor gru_forward( torch::Tensor proj_ih, torch::Tensor W_hh_T, torch::Tensor b_hh, torch::Tensor h0, torch::Tensor output, int out_stride0, int out_stride1, int out_offset, torch::Tensor h_out, int seq_len, int batch, int hidden, bool reverse) { int threads = 256; int shared_mem = hidden * sizeof(float); // only for h AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES(proj_ih.scalar_type(), "gru_forward", [&] { gru_forward_kernel<<<batch, threads, shared_mem>>>( proj_ih.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), W_hh_T.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), b_hh.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), h0.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), output.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), out_stride0, out_stride1, out_offset, h_out.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), seq_len, batch, hidden, reverse ); }); return h_out; } ``` Wait, `AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES` is good practice but might add overhead. Since we know it's float32, I can just use `float` directly. But for generality, dispatching is fine. Actually, looking at other optimized kernels, they often just use `float*` directly. I'll do that for simplicity. However, one issue: `W_hh_T` might need to be contiguous. I should ensure the transposed weights are contiguous in `__init__`. Let me also think about shared memory size. In the kernel, I allocate `extern __shared__ float smem[];` and use `hidden * sizeof(float)` bytes. For hidden=256, that's 1024 bytes. The kernel parameter `shared_mem` should be `hidden * sizeof(float) + hidden * 3 * sizeof(float)`. Wait, do I need `gh` in shared memory? In my kernel, gh is used to store the full (3*hidden) gate values so that all threads can read them in the second phase. But do I actually need shared memory for gh? Thread tid writes to gh[tid], gh[tid+hidden], gh[tid+2*hidden]. Then thread tid reads gh[i], gh[i+hidden], gh[i+2*hidden] for i = tid (if blockDim=hidden). Since each thread writes exactly the elements it reads, and both happen in the same block with __syncthreads() between, we could use registers instead! Wait, each thread computes 3 values: sum_r, sum_z, sum_n. Then in the second phase, each thread needs gh[i] for the i it handles. But thread tid handles index `i = tid` in the second phase. It needs gh[tid], gh[tid+hidden], gh[tid+2*hidden]. And these are exactly what thread tid computed! So we can completely eliminate the `gh` shared memory array! Each thread stores its 3 computed sums in local registers, and uses them directly. Let me revise: ```cuda __global__ void gru_forward_kernel( const float* proj_ih, const float* W_hh_T, const float* b_hh, const float* h0, float* output, int out_stride0, int out_stride1, int out_offset, float* h_out, int seq_len, int batch, int hidden, bool reverse) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int three_hidden = hidden * 3; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* h = smem; for (int i = tid; i < hidden; i += blockDim.x) { h[i] = h0[b * hidden + i]; } __syncthreads(); for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; t++) { int t_idx = reverse ? (seq_len - 1 - t) : t; float sum_r = 0.0f, sum_z = 0.0f, sum_n = 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < hidden; k++) { float h_k = h[k]; float w_r = W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + tid]; float w_z = W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + tid + hidden]; float w_n = W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + tid + 2 * hidden]; sum_r += h_k * w_r; sum_z += h_k * w_z; sum_n += h_k * w_n; } // Wait, we need to synchronize here because h is shared and might be updated by some threads // in the next iteration before all threads finish reading it in this iteration. Actually no, // h is only updated at the end of this block, after the loop. So no synchronization needed // between the matvec and the update, as long as all threads are in the same loop iteration. // But we DO need to make sure all threads have finished using h[k] for the matvec // before any thread updates h. That's naturally true if we do the update after // all threads have computed their sums. But there's an implicit assumption that // threads don't wrap around to the next iteration. Since it's in the same block // and all threads participate, __syncthreads() is needed before updating h, // and after updating h before the next iteration's matvec. // But we don't need __syncthreads() between the matvec and the h update // because each thread only reads h and writes to local registers for the matvec. // However, if blockDim.x > hidden, some threads won't participate in the h update. // For blockDim.x = 256 and hidden = 256, all threads update exactly one element. // Good. const float* proj_t = proj_ih + t_idx * batch * three_hidden + b * three_hidden; // For tid < hidden, compute the new h[tid] if (tid < hidden) { float gi_r = proj_t[tid]; float gi_z = proj_t[tid + hidden]; float gi_n = proj_t[tid + 2 * hidden]; float gh_r = sum_r + b_hh[tid]; float gh_z = sum_z + b_hh[tid + hidden]; float gh_n = sum_n + b_hh[tid + 2 * hidden]; float r = sigmoid(gi_r + gh_r); float z = sigmoid(gi_z + gh_z); float n = tanhf(gi_n + r * gh_n); h[tid] = (1.0f - z) * n + z * h[tid]; } __syncthreads(); // Write to output if (tid < hidden) { output[t_idx * out_stride0 + b * out_stride1 + out_offset + tid] = h[tid]; } __syncthreads(); } if (tid < hidden) { h_out[b * hidden + tid] = h[tid]; } } ``` Wait, there's a critical bug! Each thread only computes `sum_r`, `sum_z`, `sum_n` for a specific `tid`. Then thread `tid` computes `gh_r = sum_r + b_hh[tid]`. This is correct for `tid`! But in the second phase, thread `tid` reads `b_hh[tid + hidden]` and `b_hh[tid + 2 * hidden]`. Those are the biases for the z and n gates at index `tid`. And `sum_z` and `sum_n` are also for index `tid`. So it's correct. But wait: `proj_t[tid]`, `proj_t[tid + hidden]`, `proj_t[tid + 2 * hidden]` are the input projections for the r, z, n gates at index `tid`. So: - `gi_r = proj_t[tid]` - `gi_z = proj_t[tid + hidden]` - `gi_n = proj_t[tid + 2 * hidden]` Then: - `gates_r = gi_r + sum_r + b_hh[tid]` - `gates_z = gi_z + sum_z + b_hh[tid + hidden]` - `gates_n_raw = sum_n + b_hh[tid + 2 * hidden]` - `n = tanh(gi_n + r * gates_n_raw)` Yes, this is correct. But wait, do I need `__syncthreads()` after writing `h[tid]`? Yes, because the next iteration all threads read `h[k]` for all k. So we need all writes to h to complete before any thread starts reading h again. The `__syncthreads()` after updating h handles this. Similarly, writing to output doesn't need synchronization with other threads for correctness (since each timestep writes to a different t_idx in memory, and there are no other dependencies). But I added __syncthreads() for safety. Actually, the output write and the next t iteration don't depend on each other being complete across threads within the same block, since the next iteration only reads h (which was already synchronized). So the __syncthreads() after output write is unnecessary but harmless. Actually, there's a subtle issue. If `tid >= hidden`, the thread skips the h update, the output write, and the h_out write. But `__syncthreads()` requires all threads in the block to reach it. In our code, all threads reach all `__syncthreads()` because they're outside the `if (tid < hidden)` condition except for the output write. Wait, in my revised code: ```cuda if (tid < hidden) { output[...] = h[tid]; } __syncthreads(); ``` All threads reach this __syncthreads(). Good. But I removed __syncthreads() between the matvec and the h update. Is that safe? Thread tid computes sum_r/z/n using h[k] for k=0..255. At the same time, other threads in the block might be updating h if they wrapped around to the next iteration. But no, because we're still in the same `t` iteration. The loop body must be completed by ALL threads before moving to t+1. There's no `__syncthreads()` between the matvec and the h update, but there IS a `__syncthreads()` at the end of the previous iteration (and at the end of initialization). So all threads start the matvec simultaneously. The matvec only reads h (no writes). The h update writes to h but only after all threads have finished their matvec. Wait, is there any guarantee that all threads finish the matvec before any thread starts the h update? In CUDA, threads in a block execute independently unless explicitly synchronized. So thread 0 could finish its matvec quickly, reach `if (tid < hidden) { h[tid] = ... }`, and write to h[0], while thread 1 is still in the matvec reading h[0]! This is a race condition! Yes, we absolutely need `__syncthreads()` between the matvec and the h update. Revised code: ```cuda for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; t++) { int t_idx = reverse ? (seq_len - 1 - t) : t; float sum_r = 0.0f, sum_z = 0.0f, sum_n = 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < hidden; k++) { float h_k = h[k]; float w_r = W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + tid]; float w_z = W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + tid + hidden]; float w_n = W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + tid + 2 * hidden]; sum_r += h_k * w_r; sum_z += h_k * w_z; sum_n += h_k * w_n; } __syncthreads(); // Wait for all threads to finish reading h const float* proj_t = proj_ih + t_idx * batch * three_hidden + b * three_hidden; if (tid < hidden) { float gi_r = proj_t[tid]; float gi_z = proj_t[tid + hidden]; float gi_n = proj_t[tid + 2 * hidden]; float gh_r = sum_r + b_hh[tid]; float gh_z = sum_z + b_hh[tid + hidden]; float gh_n = sum_n + b_hh[tid + 2 * hidden]; float r = sigmoid(gi_r + gh_r); float z = sigmoid(gi_z + gh_z); float n = tanhf(gi_n + r * gh_n); h[tid] = (1.0f - z) * n + z * h[tid]; } __syncthreads(); // Wait for all h writes to complete if (tid < hidden) { output[t_idx * out_stride0 + b * out_stride1 + out_offset + tid] = h[tid]; } __syncthreads(); } ``` Yes, this is correct. Two extra __syncthreads() per timestep. Wait, actually we could potentially merge the output write and h update, and then just have one __syncthreads() per timestep before the matvec. Let's see: ```cuda for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; t++) { int t_idx = reverse ? (seq_len - 1 - t) : t; // ... matvec ... __syncthreads(); // ... compute new h and write to output simultaneously ... if (tid < hidden) { // compute gates... h[tid] = new_h; output[t_idx * ... + tid] = new_h; } __syncthreads(); } ``` This saves one __syncthreads() per timestep. For 512 timesteps, saving 512 syncs is 512 * ~10-20 cycles = ~5000-10000 cycles, which is negligible. But still, cleaner. Let me use the merged version: ```cuda for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; t++) { int t_idx = reverse ? (seq_len - 1 - t) : t; float sum_r = 0.0f, sum_z = 0.0f, sum_n = 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < hidden; k++) { float h_k = h[k]; float w_r = W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + tid]; float w_z = W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + tid + hidden]; float w_n = W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + tid + 2 * hidden]; sum_r += h_k * w_r; sum_z += h_k * w_z; sum_n += h_k * w_n; } __syncthreads(); const float* proj_t = proj_ih + t_idx * batch * three_hidden + b * three_hidden; if (tid < hidden) { float gi_r = proj_t[tid]; float gi_z = proj_t[tid + hidden]; float gi_n = proj_t[tid + 2 * hidden]; float gh_r = sum_r + b_hh[tid]; float gh_z = sum_z + b_hh[tid + hidden]; float gh_n = sum_n + b_hh[tid + 2 * hidden]; float r = sigmoid(gi_r + gh_r); float z = sigmoid(gi_z + gh_z); float n = tanhf(gi_n + r * gh_n); float new_h = (1.0f - z) * n + z * h[tid]; h[tid] = new_h; output[t_idx * out_stride0 + b * out_stride1 + out_offset + tid] = new_h; } __syncthreads(); } ``` Great. This is correct and has minimal synchronization. One more optimization: `h_k` is loaded from shared memory inside the loop. Since all threads read the same `h[k]` at iteration k, this is a broadcast. The compiler might optimize this, but explicitly loading it into a register is good. Also, the weight loads might benefit from `__ldg` (load from global through read-only cache), but with L2 caching and coalesced access, regular loads should be fine. I'll add `#pragma unroll` to hint it. Actually, let me consider if I should manually unroll or use the pragma. I'll use `#pragma unroll 4` for moderate unrolling. Now, for the stream optimization: to overlap forward and backward computations within a layer, I can use two CUDA streams. But in PyTorch, using streams adds complexity. Let me think about whether it's worth it. In PyTorch: ```python stream1 = torch.cuda.Stream() stream2 = torch.cuda.Stream() ``` Wait, creating streams in each forward call might be expensive. I should create them in `__init__`. But PyTorch streams can be created ahead of time. ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, ...): ... self.stream1 = torch.cuda.Stream() self.stream2 = torch.cuda.Stream() def forward(self, x, h0): ... with torch.cuda.stream(self.stream1): gru_cuda.gru_forward(...) with torch.cuda.stream(self.stream2): gru_cuda.gru_forward(...) torch.cuda.synchronize() ... ``` Wait, `torch.cuda.synchronize()` blocks the CPU until both streams finish. The next layer starts only after both directions complete. This is correct. But `torch.cuda.synchronize()` is global synchronization. Because: - Thread idx computes sum_r, sum_z, sum_n for index idx. - It computes gates for index idx: r = sigmoid(gi_r[idx] + sum_r + b_hh[idx]). - It updates h[idx]. - It writes output for idx. Revised kernel: ```cuda __global__ void gru_forward_kernel(...) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int block_size = blockDim.x; int three_hidden = hidden * 3; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* h = smem; // Load h0 cooperatively for (int i = tid; i < hidden; i += block_size) { h[i] = h0[b * hidden + i]; } __syncthreads(); for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; t++) { int t_idx = reverse ? In the inner `for (int idx = tid; idx < hidden; idx += block_size)` loop, thread tid computes multiple indices sequentially. At the end of each idx iteration, it writes to `h[idx]`. But then at the next idx iteration, it reads `h[k]` which might have been updated by another thread in the SAME timestep! Actually, do we read h[k] in the inner loop? Yes, in `for (int k = 0; k < hidden; k++)`, we read `h[k]`. If thread tid updates `h[idx1]` and then continues to `idx2`, when it computes sum for `idx2`, it reads `h[k]` which has already been updated for k = idx1 (if some thread completed that). This is wrong! The matvec must use the OLD h values from the PREVIOUS timestep. So we can't update h gradually as we compute! We must compute all matvecs using the OLD h, and only then update all h values. This brings us back to needing either: 1. All threads finish their matvec loops before any thread updates h, OR 2. Each thread computes all its indices' matvecs first, stores them locally, then updates h and output. With option 2, each thread can handle multiple indices. In the matvec loop, it never reads h values that it has already updated, because it doesn't update h until all its matvecs are done. However, it might read h[k] that another thread has updated if that other thread finished its matvecs and started updating. To prevent this, we need `__syncthreads()` between the matvec phase and the update phase. So the correct structure is: ```cuda for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; t++) { int t_idx = reverse ? (seq_len - 1 - t) : t; // Phase 1: All threads compute matvecs using current h // Each thread computes sums for indices idx = tid, tid+block_size, ... // Store sums locally in registers // Since there can be many indices, we can't store all in registers if hidden > block_size // For hidden=256 and block_size=256, each thread computes exactly 1 set of sums // For hidden > block_size, we'd need local storage // Wait, if each thread handles ceil(hidden/block_size) indices, and hidden=512, block=256, // each thread handles 2 indices. We can store 6 floats (3 sums * 2 indices) in registers. // For hidden=1024, block=256, 4 indices, 12 floats. Fine. // But what about hidden=5120? That's 20 indices, 60 floats. Register pressure. // Alternatively, use shared memory to store the computed gh values. // gh_shared[3*hidden] - each thread writes its computed values, then all read them. // But writing to shared memory and reading back is basically what we want. ``` Actually, for the general case where hidden could be large, storing all gh values in shared memory might be necessary. But shared memory is limited to 227 KB. 3*hidden*4 bytes = 12*hidden bytes. For hidden=1024, that's 12 KB. For hidden=10240, that's 120 KB. For hidden=20000, that's 240 KB, exceeding shared memory. (seq_len - 1 - t) : t; // Compute sum_r, sum_z, sum_n for all indices handled by this thread for (int idx = tid; idx < hidden; idx += block_size) { float sum_r = 0.0f, sum_z = 0.0f, sum_n = 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < hidden; k++) { float h_k = h[k]; sum_r += h_k * W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + idx]; sum_z += h_k * W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + idx + hidden]; sum_n += h_k * W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + idx + 2 * hidden]; } // Store in shared memory smem[tid] = sum_r; smem[tid + block_size] = sum_z; smem[tid + 2 * block_size] = sum_n; } __syncthreads(); // Now update h and output using the stored sums for (int idx = tid; idx < hidden; idx += block_size) { float sum_r = smem[idx]; float sum_z = smem[idx + block_size]; float sum_n = smem[idx + 2 * block_size]; const float* proj_t = ...; // ... compute gates and new h ... h[idx] = new_h; output[...] = new_h; } __syncthreads(); } ``` Wait, this shared memory approach assumes idx maps directly to smem[idx], which only works if each idx is handled by exactly one thread. If hidden=512 and block_size=256, idx=0 is handled by thread 0, idx=256 is handled by thread 0 again (in the second iteration). Actually, the simplest general approach is to allocate shared memory for ALL indices: `gh_shared[3 * hidden]` if it fits. Or we can compute and update in two phases: 1. Then all threads update the hidden states cooperatively For the parallel matvec with 256 threads and hidden=H: - We have H outputs. We can process them in chunks. - Each thread can compute a partial dot product for a specific (k, idx) pair. Actually, the most general and clean approach is: ```cuda __global__ void gru_forward_kernel(...) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int block_size = blockDim.x; int three_hidden = hidden * 3; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* h = smem; float* sums = smem + hidden; // Load h0 for (int i = tid; i < hidden; i += block_size) h[i] = h0[b * hidden + i]; __syncthreads(); for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; t++) { int t_idx = reverse ? (seq_len - 1 - t) : t; // Phase 1: Compute sums = W_hh_T.T @ h = W_hh @ h // We need sums[0..3*hidden-1] // sums[idx] = sum_k h[k] * W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + idx] // Parallelize over idx: each thread handles a subset of idx for (int idx = tid; idx < three_hidden; idx += block_size) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < hidden; k++) { sum += h[k] * W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + idx]; } sums[idx] = sum; } __syncthreads(); // Phase 2: Update h and output const float* proj_t = proj_ih + t_idx * batch * three_hidden + b * three_hidden; for (int idx = tid; idx < hidden; idx += block_size) { float gi_r = proj_t[idx]; float gi_z = proj_t[idx + hidden]; float gi_n = proj_t[idx + 2 * hidden]; float gh_r = sums[idx] + b_hh[idx]; float gh_z = sums[idx + hidden] + b_hh[idx + hidden]; float gh_n = sums[idx + 2 * hidden] + b_hh[idx + 2 * hidden]; float r = sigmoid(gi_r + gh_r); float z = sigmoid(gi_z + gh_z); float n = tanhf(gi_n + r * gh_n); float new_h = (1.0f - z) * n + z * h[idx]; h[idx] = new_h; output[t_idx * out_stride0 + b * out_stride1 + out_offset + idx] = new_h; } __syncthreads(); } for (int i = tid; i < hidden; i += block_size) { h_out[b * hidden + i] = h[i]; } } ``` Shared memory needed: hidden + 3*hidden = 4*hidden floats. For hidden=256: 1024 floats = 4KB. Very small. For hidden=1024: 4096 floats = 16KB. Small. For hidden=10000: 40000 floats = 160KB. Still under 227KB. For hidden=15000: 60000 floats = 240KB. Exceeds 227KB. So this works for hidden up to about 14000. That's very general and more than enough. Block size: I can use 256 threads to maximize parallelism. Or even 512/1024. For hidden=256, three_hidden=768, each thread computes 3 indices, with 768/256 = 3 exactly. Each thread does 3 dot products of length 256 = 768 FMAs. - In Phase 2, each thread computes `hidden / 256 = 1` index for hidden=256. This works well. And the __syncthreads() ensures no race conditions. For the weight access pattern in Phase 1: thread tid computes indices `tid`, `tid+256`, `tid+512` (for hidden=256). Actually, there's a subtle performance consideration: multiple threads in a warp read W_hh_T with the same k but different tid. These are 768 elements apart for different k. Wait no, for fixed k, W_hh_T[k*768 + tid] for tid consecutive are consecutive. For tid=0,1,2,... they read k*768+0, k*768+1, k*768+2,... And for the three accesses (tid, tid+256, tid+512), the first is coalesced, the second is coalesced, the third is coalesced. But they are back-to-back, so the memory controller might need to handle three separate transactions per k. We could potentially restructure so each thread computes only one index per inner loop, and the outer loop over the 3 indices handles the parallelism. But then we'd need to launch more threads or do more iterations. Not sure if it helps. Actually, we can unroll the 3 accesses manually: ```cuda #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < hidden; k++) { float h_k = h[k]; sum_r += h_k * W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + idx]; sum_z += h_k * W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + idx + hidden]; sum_n += h_k * W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + idx + 2 * hidden]; } ``` The compiler can optimize this well, potentially keeping the 3 weight loads in flight. For hidden > 256, `idx` takes values `tid, tid+block_size, tid+2*block_size, ...`. The three accesses for a given `idx` are coalesced. The next `idx` in the outer loop is `idx + block_size`. For `idx = tid + block_size`, the access is `W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + tid + block_size]`. For consecutive threads in a warp, these are also consecutive. So coalesced. Great, the coalescing works for any hidden and block_size. Let me now write the complete code. Wait, one more thing: I said `block_size = 256`. What if hidden is not a multiple of 256? Now let me draft the Python code with the CUDA inline. One more thought: should I use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` at the top of the file? Yes. The CUDA code will be compiled when the module is loaded. However, `load_inline` caches the compiled extension. If we modify the code, we might need a different `name` or it might use the cached version. For the evaluation, the code is executed fresh, so it should compile correctly. Let me write the full code now: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F # Compile the CUDA kernel cuda_code = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __device__ __forceinline__ float sigmoid(float x) { return 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x)); } __global__ void gru_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ proj_ih, const float* __restrict__ W_hh_T, const float* __restrict__ b_hh, const float* __restrict__ h0, float* __restrict__ output, int out_stride0, int out_stride1, int out_offset, float* __restrict__ h_out, int seq_len, int batch, int hidden, bool reverse) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int block_size = blockDim.x; int three_hidden = hidden * 3; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* h = smem; float* sums = smem + hidden; // Load initial hidden state for (int i = tid; i < hidden; i += block_size) { h[i] = h0[b * hidden + i]; } __syncthreads(); for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; t++) { int t_idx = reverse ? (seq_len - 1 - t) : t; // Phase 1: Compute W_hh @ h using pre-transposed weights for (int idx = tid; idx < three_hidden; idx += block_size) { float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll 4 for (int k = 0; k < hidden; k++) { sum += h[k] * W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + idx]; } sums[idx] = sum; } __syncthreads(); // Phase 2: Compute gates, new hidden state, and write output const float* proj_t = proj_ih + t_idx * batch * three_hidden + b * three_hidden; for (int idx = tid; idx < hidden; idx += block_size) { float gi_r = proj_t[idx]; float gi_z = proj_t[idx + hidden]; float gi_n = proj_t[idx + 2 * hidden]; float gh_r = sums[idx] + b_hh[idx]; float gh_z = sums[idx + hidden] + b_hh[idx + hidden]; float gh_n = sums[idx + 2 * hidden] + b_hh[idx + 2 * hidden]; float r = sigmoid(gi_r + gh_r); float z = sigmoid(gi_z + gh_z); float n = tanhf(gi_n + r * gh_n); float new_h = (1.0f - z) * n + z * h[idx]; h[idx] = new_h; output[t_idx * out_stride0 + b * out_stride1 + out_offset + idx] = new_h; } __syncthreads(); } // Write final hidden state for (int i = tid; i < hidden; i += block_size) { h_out[b * hidden + i] = h[i]; } } torch::Tensor gru_forward( torch::Tensor proj_ih, torch::Tensor W_hh_T, torch::Tensor b_hh, torch::Tensor h0, torch::Tensor output, int out_stride0, int out_stride1, int out_offset, torch::Tensor h_out, int seq_len, int batch, int hidden, bool reverse) { int threads = 256; int shared_mem_size = (hidden + 3 * hidden) * sizeof(float); gru_forward_kernel<<<batch, threads, shared_mem_size>>>( proj_ih.data_ptr<float>(), W_hh_T.data_ptr<float>(), b_hh.data_ptr<float>(), h0.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), out_stride0, out_stride1, out_offset, h_out.data_ptr<float>(), seq_len, batch, hidden, reverse ); return h_out; } ''' gru_cuda = torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline( name="gru_cuda_forward", cuda_sources=cuda_code, functions=["gru_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def self.batch_first else x.size(1) device = x.device current = x h_n_list = [] for layer in range(self.num_layers): h0_f = h0[layer * 2] h0_b = h0[layer * 2 + 1] # Input projections proj_ih_f = 0:self.hidden_size]`. This creates a view with different strides. When passed to the CUDA kernel, the `.data_ptr()` will return the base address of `output_buf` plus the slice offset, and the strides will be `output_buf.stride(0)` and `output_buf.stride(1)`. The kernel's `out_offset` parameter handles the offset within the last dimension. But what about `out_stride0` and `out_stride1`? For `output_buf[:, :, 0:hidden]`, the slice has the same strides as `output_buf` because slicing the last dimension with 0:hidden doesn't change the strides of the first two dimensions. So `output_buf[:, :, 0:hidden].stride(0) == output_buf.stride(0)` and similarly for stride(1). Good. And `output_buf[:, :, 0:hidden].data_ptr()` returns the pointer to element (0,0,0), which is `output_buf.data_ptr() + 0`. The `out_offset` is 0. Good. For `output_buf[:, :, hidden:2*hidden]`, `.data_ptr()` returns `output_buf.data_ptr() + hidden * sizeof(float)`. But wait, the element offset is `hidden`, not `hidden * output_buf.stride(2)`. Since `output_buf.stride(2) = 1`, the byte offset is `hidden * sizeof(float)`. However, in our kernel, `out_offset` is passed as an integer offset in the last dimension, and we compute: `output_ptr + t_idx * out_stride0 + b * out_stride1 + out_offset + idx`. If `output_ptr` is `output_buf[:, :, hidden:].data_ptr()`, then the actual base address already includes the `hidden` offset. And then `out_offset = hidden` adds another `hidden` offset, giving `2*hidden`. That's wrong! Let me fix this. I should pass the base pointer of `output_buf` and set `out_offset` appropriately, or pass the slice pointer with `out_offset=0`. Option 1: Pass `output_buf.data_ptr()` directly and set `out_offset`: - Forward: `output_ptr = output_buf.data_ptr()`, `out_offset = 0` - Backward: `output_ptr = output_buf.data_ptr()`, `out_offset = hidden` But in my Python code above, I'm passing the sliced tensor to `gru_forward`, which means the data_ptr already has the offset. Both kernels write to different regions of the same `output_buf` tensor. Wait, but do the two kernel calls run in parallel or sequentially? In the default stream, they run sequentially. That's fine for correctness. We could use streams for parallel execution but it's not necessary for correctness. Also, I need to ensure that `output_buf.stride(0)` and `output_buf.stride(1)` are passed as integers representing the element strides. In PyTorch, `tensor.stride()` returns element strides in elements, not bytes. And in the kernel, since we're doing pointer arithmetic on `float*`, using element strides is correct. Let me verify: `output_buf` has shape (seq_len, batch, 2*hidden). - `output_buf.stride(0)` = batch * 2 * hidden (elements to skip for next seq position) - `output_buf.stride(1)` = 2 * hidden (elements to skip for next batch) - `output_buf.stride(2)` = 1 In the kernel: `output[t_idx * out_stride0 + b * out_stride1 + out_offset + idx]` For forward: `out_offset = 0`, so we access element `[t_idx, b, idx]`. But the test uses `torch.rand` which defaults to float32. Good. Now let me think about correctness with respect to the exact PyTorch GRU implementation. I need to be very careful about whether `nn.GRU` uses the "updated" GRU formulation or another variant. PyTorch's GRU documentation: ``` r_t = sigmoid(W_ir x_t + b_ir + W_hr h_{t-1} + b_hr) z_t = sigmoid(W_iz x_t + b_iz + W_hz h_{t-1} + b_hz) n_t = tanh(W_in x_t + b_in + r_t * (W_hn h_{t-1} + b_hn)) h_t = (1 - z_t) * n_t + z_t * h_{t-1} ``` Wait, there's a discrepancy in some sources about whether the bias is added before or after the recurrence. Let me check PyTorch source. From PyTorch source, the GRU computation is at: https://github.com/pytorch/pytorch/blob/main/aten/src/ATen/native/cuda/RNN.cu Or more relevant, the Python implementation in torch/nn/modules/rnn.py and the functional in torch/nn/functional.py. Actually, PyTorch's GRUCell does: ```python gi = F.linear(input, weight_ih, bias_ih) gh = F.linear(hidden, weight_hh, bias_hh) i_r, i_z, i_n = gi.chunk(3, 1) h_r, h_z, h_n = gh.chunk(3, 1) resetgate = sigmoid(i_r + h_r) inputgate = sigmoid(i_z + h_z) newgate = tanh(i_n + resetgate * h_n) hy = newgate + inputgate * (hidden - newgate) ``` Expand: - `gi = W_ih @ x + b_ih` - `gh = W_hh @ h + b_hh` - `resetgate = sigmoid(gi_r + gh_r)` where gi_r is the first chunk of gi and gh_r is the first chunk of gh. - `inputgate = sigmoid(gi_z + gh_z)` - `newgate = tanh(gi_n + resetgate * gh_n)` where gi_n is the third chunk of gi and gh_n is the third chunk of gh. - `hy = newgate + inputgate * (hidden - newgate)` = `(1 - inputgate) * newgate + inputgate * hidden` Notice: `hy = newgate + inputgate * (hidden - newgate)` expands to `(1 - inputgate) * newgate + inputgate * hidden`. This is exactly what I have: - `r = resetgate` - `z = inputgate` - `n = newgate` - `new_h = (1 - z) * n + z * h` And: - `gh_r = sums_r + b_hh_r` where `sums_r` is the matvec result for the r gate. - `gi_r = proj_r`. - `r = sigmoid(gi_r + gh_r)`. ✓ But wait, `gh_r = W_hh_r @ h + b_hh_r`. My sums[idx] computes `W_hh[idx, :] @ h` where idx corresponds to the idx-th row of W_hh. But W_hh is transposed: `W_hh_T[k * three_hidden + idx]` corresponds to `W_hh[idx, k]`. So `sum over k W_hh[idx, k] * h[k]` = `(W_hh @ h)[idx]`. Then `gh_r = sums_r + b_hh_r`. But hold on, `sums_r` for idx is `(W_hh @ h)[idx]`, which equals the idx-th row of W_hh dotted with h. And `b_hh_r` is `b_hh[idx]`. So `gh_r = (W_hh @ h)[idx] + b_hh[idx]`. But `W_hh @ h + b_hh` gives a vector of length 3*hidden. The first hidden elements are `gh_r`, so `b_hh[idx]` is indeed the bias for the r gate at index idx. Similarly for z and n. For proj_ih: `proj_ih_f = F.linear(current, W_ih, b_ih)`. This is `current @ W_ih.T + b_ih`. The result at idx is `(current @ W_ih.T)[idx] + b_ih[idx]`. For `proj_t = proj_ih + t_idx * ... + b * ...`, `proj_t[idx]` is `gi_r(idx)` for `idx < hidden`, `gi_z(idx)` for `hidden <= idx < 2*hidden`, etc. So my implementation matches PyTorch exactly. But there's still one subtlety: what order are the gates packed in `W_ih` and `W_hh`? PyTorch packs them as reset, update, new (in that order). Is this always true? From PyTorch source, the linear algebra in GRU uses `weights.chunk(3, 0)` for the weight matrices. So the first dimension is split into 3 equal parts. The order is reset, update, new. This is consistent with what I assumed. So my indexing is correct: - 0..hidden-1: reset - hidden..2*hidden-1: update - 2*hidden..3*hidden-1: new Great. Alright, one more thing: should I add `torch.cuda.synchronize()` after each kernel call or after each layer? For correctness, inside a single stream, the operations are already ordered. Since the CPU doesn't depend on the kernel results until it needs to launch the next layer's kernels, and the next layer's kernels depend on the output buffer being ready, the implicit stream ordering handles it. But wait, there's a potential issue. In my Python code: ```python gru_cuda.gru_forward(...) # forward direction gru_cuda.gru_forward(...) # backward direction # Then current = output_buf for next layer ``` Both kernel calls are in the same default stream, so they execute sequentially. The second kernel starts after the first finishes. The next layer's `F.linear(current, ...)` happens after both kernels finish because everything is in the same stream. So no explicit synchronization is needed. Now, let me consider whether I should use a single kernel launch for both directions. It would save one kernel launch per layer. Actually, nvcc generates host stubs for __global__ functions. If you compile the kernel in a .cu file, and want to call it from a .cpp file, you can use a wrapper function in the .cu file that calls the kernel, and then call the wrapper from the .cpp file.
